<div id="review_154789154" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/154789154" />
    <a title="Elizabeth (Alaska)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2026178-elizabeth-alaska"><img alt="Elizabeth (Alaska)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1304966095p2/2026178.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/154789154?book_show_action=true">Jul 02, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Elizabeth (Alaska)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Elizabeth (Alaska)" href="/user/show/2026178-elizabeth-alaska">Elizabeth (Alaska)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780394442297" href="/book/show/1026696.The_Reivers">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2026178-elizabeth-alaska?shelf=author-faulkner">author-faulkner</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2026178-elizabeth-alaska?shelf=awards-pulitzer">awards-pulitzer</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2026178-elizabeth-alaska?shelf=5-star-reads">5-star-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2026178-elizabeth-alaska?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2026178-elizabeth-alaska?shelf=mid-20th-century">mid-20th-century</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer154789154" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16002461117597423688">This has the subtitle "A Reminiscence" and, as Faulkner's last novel, when I saw that I thought perhaps it was somewhat autobiographical. Then, the opening line: <blockquote>Grandfather Said: This is the kind of a man Boon Hogganbeck was. Hung on the wall, it could have been his epitaph, like a Bertillon chart or a police poster; any cop in north Mississippi would have arrested him out of any crowd after merely reading the date.</blockquote>So, instead it is the reminiscence of a man who is now a grandfather, (probably </span>
  <span id="freeText16002461117597423688" style="display:none">This has the subtitle "A Reminiscence" and, as Faulkner's last novel, when I saw that I thought perhaps it was somewhat autobiographical. Then, the opening line: <blockquote>Grandfather Said: This is the kind of a man Boon Hogganbeck was. Hung on the wall, it could have been his epitaph, like a Bertillon chart or a police poster; any cop in north Mississippi would have arrested him out of any crowd after merely reading the date.</blockquote>So, instead it is the reminiscence of a man who is now a grandfather, (probably telling his grandson) of events in 1905 when he was eleven years old. The description of this being a comic novel is accurate. Faulkner also spent some time as a screenwriter and I could see such a movie, even though I've never seen the film. I just went to look and see that one of my very favorite actors, Steven McQueen, played Boon. He was cast perfectly, I think. <br /><br />In my mind's eye, I could see Faulkner writing this. He became the grandfather telling this story. He lived it as it filled the page. And so, too, did I live the story as I read. The trip to Memphis is eventful. And who takes an 11-year old boy to a whorehouse? But it is even better than that. I have a pretty good vocabulary, but somehow I missed the meaning of the title, which I found on the back flap of my copy: <em>reive (reave) take away by stealth or force; plunder.</em> It isn't just Boon and Lucius on this trip, but also Ned, a negro employee of Lucius' own grandfather, has stowed away. Ned was my favorite character. Boon saw the opportunity to "use" the car for 4 days while Lucius' parents and grandparents were away, but it was Ned who would plot to take full advantage of that opportunity. <br /><br />Before I finished this, I spent a bit of time here on GR making sure that I had all of Faulkner's fiction on any of my wishlists, noting whether I needed to buy them in digital format, or whether they are available from my library. I have enjoyed my time spent with other Faulkner titles, but this finally convinced me I would like to read everything. Yes, some of his novels are challenging, and few of them are comic. But he's just too good to pass up.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16002461117597423688" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_125723897" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/125723897" />
    <a title="Erik" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/795024-erik"><img alt="Erik" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1530815346p2/795024.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/125723897?book_show_action=true">Oct 11, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Erik" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Erik" href="/user/show/795024-erik">Erik</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780394442297" href="/book/show/1026696.The_Reivers">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/795024-erik?shelf=did-not-finish">did-not-finish</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer125723897" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17611293817885278381">The first 50+ pages was like listening to someone tell a story with so many tangents and sentences so long that I forget where it was even headed to being with. I understand the style was part of the story, but I don't like hearing a story told in that manner in real life, so it lost its novelty quickly. I laughed out loud once, but the rest of the humor never even got a smile. The only reason I can see this won a Pulitzer is because of the year it was published and the racial commentary (not a </span>
  <span id="freeText17611293817885278381" style="display:none">The first 50+ pages was like listening to someone tell a story with so many tangents and sentences so long that I forget where it was even headed to being with. I understand the style was part of the story, but I don't like hearing a story told in that manner in real life, so it lost its novelty quickly. I laughed out loud once, but the rest of the humor never even got a smile. The only reason I can see this won a Pulitzer is because of the year it was published and the racial commentary (not a main theme, but prevalent). If the same book were published now (by an unknown), I don't think it would garner so much as a nod. I started skimming with 50 pages to go, not really caring much about the outcome. This book is definitely not for me.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17611293817885278381" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

She was a longtime William Faulkner lover and just couldn't stand the thought that she had read all his books. So she saved this one--his last, written in 1962 and a winner of the Pulitzer Prize--to read (or have read to her) on her deathbed, whenever that day came.   <span id="freeText7540241394198026107" style="display:none">The former rector of our church recently died. She was a longtime William Faulkner lover and just couldn't stand the thought that she had read all his books. So she saved this one--his last, written in 1962 and a winner of the Pulitzer Prize--to read (or have read to her) on her deathbed, whenever that day came. Tragically, she died suddenly of a heart attack and never got to read it. A few of us at our church are reading it "for her." And she would have loved this book! So will you, especially if you have any "Southern" in you. This book is unlike other Faulkner books. It's very accessible and actually funny. The moral: Don't save books for later. If you really want to read something, do it. Now.</span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/341449587?book_show_action=true">Jun 02, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="K.M. Weiland" class="user" itemprop="url" name="K.M. Weiland" href="/user/show/2971567-k-m-weiland">K.M. Weiland</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer341449587" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7941429876452873203">I find it interesting so many people found this book incomprehensible. This easily the *most* comprehensible of any of Faulkner's writings. It's also the most likable, the most charming, and the only one of his books I can say I honestly enjoyed all the way through. It's not as self-conscious, artsy, or convoluted as most of his other works, and because of that very thing, I'd say it offers more depth than even his "deep" books. Here, we actually find a cast of primarily likable characters whose</span>
  <span id="freeText7941429876452873203" style="display:none">I find it interesting so many people found this book incomprehensible. This easily the *most* comprehensible of any of Faulkner's writings. It's also the most likable, the most charming, and the only one of his books I can say I honestly enjoyed all the way through. It's not as self-conscious, artsy, or convoluted as most of his other works, and because of that very thing, I'd say it offers more depth than even his "deep" books. Here, we actually find a cast of primarily likable characters whose adventures are all the more engaging and funny for the fact that their very ridiculousness makes them seem possible. I'm glad I stuck with Faulkner long enough to read this one.</span>

<div id="review_168612979" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/168612979" />
    <a title="James" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/426277-james"><img alt="James" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1584954318p2/426277.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/168612979?book_show_action=true">May 16, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="James" class="user" itemprop="url" name="James" href="/user/show/426277-james">James</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/426277-james?shelf=pulitzer-prize">pulitzer-prize</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/426277-james?shelf=american-lit">american-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/426277-james?shelf=nobel-prize-author">nobel-prize-author</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/426277-james?shelf=lp2010s">lp2010s</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer168612979" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10286589423027515788"><i>The Reivers</i>, written at the end of William Faulkner's life, is a picaresque tale of a young boy's coming of age. There is a certain resemblance to aspects of <i>Huckleberry Finn</i> in the adventures and friendships of young Lucius Priest. Lucius, an eleven year old boy is sensitive and intelligent, but innocent of the rougher side of life and ready for adventure when Boon Hogganbeck, a simple man, and Ned William McCaslin Jefferson Missippi (a Negro referred to as Ned) steal Lucius' grandfather's car </span>
  <span id="freeText10286589423027515788" style="display:none"><i>The Reivers</i>, written at the end of William Faulkner's life, is a picaresque tale of a young boy's coming of age. There is a certain resemblance to aspects of <i>Huckleberry Finn</i> in the adventures and friendships of young Lucius Priest. Lucius, an eleven year old boy is sensitive and intelligent, but innocent of the rougher side of life and ready for adventure when Boon Hogganbeck, a simple man, and Ned William McCaslin Jefferson Missippi (a Negro referred to as Ned) steal Lucius' grandfather's car and head off for Memphis with Lucius in tow. The presence of cars in this early twentieth-century tale suggests the many changes in society that would occur later in the century. This story seems to be suspended in time, sometimes a time that feels like it never was, except in someone's imagination.<br />The encounters Lucius has over the next few days are as exciting as those of Huck and they lead him to meditate on his own innocence and its loss. Early on he recognizes this thinking, "You see? I was doing the best I could. My trouble was, the tools I had to use. the innocence and the ignorance: I not only didn't have strength and knowledge, I didn't even have time enough."(p 55) Later in their adventures, after Ned has traded the stolen car for a race horse, Lucius reflects further, "It was too late. Maybe yesterday, while I was still a child, but not now. I knew too much, had seen too much. I was a child no longer now; innocence and childhood were forever lost, forever gone from me."(p 175)<br /><br />The novel is not all serious moments of reflection like these; for there is the excitement of the horse races, Lucius' friendship with the Corrie, the prostitute, and his experience with the negro old Possum and his family. The adventures, while real for Lucius, seem to exist in a fairy tale world as the fun overshadows any sense of danger. Through it all there are just enough ties to Faulkner's earlier work through genealogy and character (Ned was present in several tales of <i>Go Down, Moses</i>) to make this a fitting bookend to his career. In it you see a mature author brilliantly developing yet another view of a young boy's coming of age.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10286589423027515788" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_95097847" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/95097847" />
    <a title="terrycojones" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/550761-terrycojones"><img alt="terrycojones" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1212740225p2/550761.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/95097847?book_show_action=true">Mar 21, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="terrycojones" class="user" itemprop="url" name="terrycojones" href="/user/show/550761-terrycojones">terrycojones</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer95097847" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18322235925016335813">I really enjoyed The Reivers. I wasn't expecting to because I'd read in several places that it was a "comic masterpiece" and I didn't really like Faulkner's attempts at humor (e.g., the attempted comic scenes as Soldier's Pay degenerates into farce). But his dark humor - that's another thing altogether, and Faulkner has a deft touch.<br/><br/>He's also a master at the child's-eye-view of proceedings in the adult world. You can see this in many places in Faulkner (e.g., in Barn Burning, in Intruder In The </span>
  <span id="freeText18322235925016335813" style="display:none">I really enjoyed The Reivers. I wasn't expecting to because I'd read in several places that it was a "comic masterpiece" and I didn't really like Faulkner's attempts at humor (e.g., the attempted comic scenes as Soldier's Pay degenerates into farce). But his dark humor - that's another thing altogether, and Faulkner has a deft touch.<br /><br />He's also a master at the child's-eye-view of proceedings in the adult world. You can see this in many places in Faulkner (e.g., in Barn Burning, in Intruder In The Dust, and of course in The Sound &amp; The Fury). The Reivers is told through the eyes of Lucius Priest, and Faulkner nails it. Of course the child is impossibly mature in a sense (I'd almost say it *has* to be that way, but The Sound &amp; The Fury prevents me), but he's still a child. The Reivers also features one of Faulkner's lovable, intelligent, and wise black characters, in this case Ned. He's reminiscent of Lucas in Intruder in the Dust, and of Sam Fathers in The Bear. Present from The Bear we have the abysmal marksman, Boon Hogganbeck, one of the main characters of The Reivers. There's also a big role for Miss Reba, the Memphis whorehouse proprietress (from Sanctuary) and her nasty foul-mouthed man, Mr Binford (who in Sanctuary is deceased or gone, and who has been replaced by a dog of the same name).<br /><br />The Reivers is Faulkner at the top of his game, at least in this kind of novel, and I think is an answer to a dilemma I've had for ages: what's the best first Faulkner novel to recommend to someone who's read none of them? As I Lay Dying had been my choice until now. There's plenty in The Reivers for all comers.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18322235925016335813" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1367362927" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1367362927" />
    <a title="Jim Peterson" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/24498353-jim-peterson"><img alt="Jim Peterson" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1381082195p2/24498353.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1367362927?book_show_action=true">Aug 18, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jim Peterson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jim Peterson" href="/user/show/24498353-jim-peterson">Jim Peterson</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/24498353-jim-peterson?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/24498353-jim-peterson?shelf=english">english</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/24498353-jim-peterson?shelf=pulitzer">pulitzer</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/24498353-jim-peterson?shelf=nobel-prize-winners">nobel-prize-winners</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1367362927" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9956159712343934885">If you don’t want to bother with my ramblings, just skip to the last paragraph for my opinion of the book :-)<br/><br/>I live in Europe, and several times in the past when someone discovered I was (and still am) an American, they might go on to talk about some American author and what did I think about him or her. I would either admit I hadn’t read the author in question or pretend as if I had – both of which options would be rather awkward. And so, I’ve decided to remedy that situation and have been spen</span>
  <span id="freeText9956159712343934885" style="display:none">If you don’t want to bother with my ramblings, just skip to the last paragraph for my opinion of the book :-)<br /><br />I live in Europe, and several times in the past when someone discovered I was (and still am) an American, they might go on to talk about some American author and what did I think about him or her. I would either admit I hadn’t read the author in question or pretend as if I had – both of which options would be rather awkward. And so, I’ve decided to remedy that situation and have been spending most of my reading time trying to catch up on American authors. Hence Faulkner. <br /><br />Faulkner is not easy to read – at first, at least. He writes extremely long sentences, many of which would be marked with red ink by English teachers as “run-on sentences”. Reading Faulkner felt like my first attempts at reading German. You read, re-read, push on, not really understanding everything, but knowing that soon the blurry picture in your head will come more and more into focus. <br /><br />I read up on Faulkner’s style: stream of consciousness. Ah hah. And IF that’s what it is (a stream), then what the h— was I doing re-reading anything? Why not just go with the flow? And that’s what I did. I just read it quickly, not worrying about the details, and soon everything became clear – and I started loving it. Really, really loving it.<br /><br />The Reivers is a fantastic story, the writing is like nothing I’ve ever seen (probably it wouldn’t get past the editors today) and the story transported me to the old south. I have already bought three more of Faulkner’s books, but I think I’m going to read something short and simple first…<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9956159712343934885" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer7273302399646803089">I had some trouble to fight my way through this novel.<br/><br/>The story is actually quite interesting and amusing: The three heroes, 11 yo Lucius, Boon Hogganbeck and "Uncle" Ned pilfer the car of Lucius' grandfather and want to travel for four days to the "big city". You have to understand that at the time in which the story takes place only very few cars (that were called automobiles back then) were seen on the so-called roads in the Southern U.S. and a journey this long represents a considerable chal</span>
  <span id="freeText7273302399646803089" style="display:none">I had some trouble to fight my way through this novel.<br><br>The story is actually quite interesting and amusing: The three heroes, 11 yo Lucius, Boon Hogganbeck and "Uncle" Ned pilfer the car of Lucius' grandfather and want to travel for four days to the "big city". You have to understand that at the time in which the story takes place only very few cars (that were called automobiles back then) were seen on the so-called roads in the Southern U.S. and a journey this long represents a considerable challenge to man and machine.<br><br>It is the description of this journey from Jefferson, MS to Memphis, TN, which I liked best about the book. The rest is somehow plowed under the too complicated structured monsters of sentences. I generally have no problem with long sentences, as long as they come along harmonically/melodically and do not disturb my flow of reading. Here it was often not the case. <br><br>THE REIVERS is the last novel by William Faulkner and also the first one I've read. After reading I'm not sure if I want to deal with more of Faulkner's works in the near future. After all I'm reading novels mostly for fun and entertainment.<br><br>Rating 2½ of 5 stars.<br><br><a href="http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc-sa/3.0/" rel="nofollow noopener">

<div id="review_563911079" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/563911079" />
    <a title="Justin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1278726-justin"><img alt="Justin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1360898899p2/1278726.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/563911079?book_show_action=true">Mar 17, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Justin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Justin" href="/user/show/1278726-justin">Justin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer563911079" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16730485295300894260">This was my fourth and final Faulkner novel for the month of March. I have to admit that I have a bit of Faulkner fatigue which in all transparency could have impacted my rating. This is Faulkner's most accessible novel - a Huck Finn style coming of age story that is fun, exciting, and subtle in its commentary on race and manhood. It has almost no resemblance to anything else he has written. It's really quite a tribute to Faulkner's ability that he can write across such diverse genre's while sti</span>
  <span id="freeText16730485295300894260" style="display:none">This was my fourth and final Faulkner novel for the month of March. I have to admit that I have a bit of Faulkner fatigue which in all transparency could have impacted my rating. This is Faulkner's most accessible novel - a Huck Finn style coming of age story that is fun, exciting, and subtle in its commentary on race and manhood. It has almost no resemblance to anything else he has written. It's really quite a tribute to Faulkner's ability that he can write across such diverse genre's while still maintaining a core set of truths that he sets out to convey.<br /><br />I won't go into the details of the story which are available in the Good Reads overview. Suffice to say it was enjoyable and I was particularly impressed with Faulkner's treatment of race - which was significantly more subtle than in past novels - particularly the noble characters of Ned and Uncle Parshum. However, overall, I was less impressed with this than other Faulkner novels - not because it was more accessible, but rather because I just never really connected with it in the same way that I did with Absalom, Absalom! The fact that this won the Pulitzer Prize probably says something about critics ability to understand greatness at the time that it arises (rather than as a lifetime achievement award at the end).</span>
  <a data-text-id="16730485295300894260" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer5420983867215442787">Some journeys undertaken in one’s youth make an indelible imprint on the entire life… And <i>The Reivers</i> is a story of one such decisive journey.<br/>“Then Grandfather bought that automobile and Boon found his soul’s mate… My grandfather didn’t want an automobile at all; he was forced to buy one. A banker, president of the older Bank of Jefferson, the first bank in Yoknapatawpha County, he believed then and right on to his death many years afterward, by which time everybody else even in Yoknapatawpha Co</span>
  <span id="freeText5420983867215442787" style="display:none">Some journeys undertaken in one’s youth make an indelible imprint on the entire life… And <i>The Reivers</i> is a story of one such decisive journey.<br />“Then Grandfather bought that automobile and Boon found his soul’s mate… My grandfather didn’t want an automobile at all; he was forced to buy one. A banker, president of the older Bank of Jefferson, the first bank in Yoknapatawpha County, he believed then and right on to his death many years afterward, by which time everybody else even in Yoknapatawpha County had realised that the automobile had come to stay, that the motor vehicle was an insolvent phenomenon like last night’s toadstool and, like the fungus, would vanish with tomorrow’s sun.”<br />“A horse would a been different. Even if you hadn’t even paid a hundred dollars for a horse you’d a had me out there at daylight lunging him on a rope just to keep his guts working.”<br />Automobile and horse – the future and the past… The novel is also a collision of two eras – the left behind era of nature and the forthcoming era of machine.</span>

<div id="review_2454411461" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2173845798940883148">Not my favorite Faulkner, but certainly funnier and more light-hearted than most (a solid 3.5). It started off rather strong and comically with quite the memorable cast of characters, but the pace seemed kind of uneven about halfway through and the ending wrapped things up a little too neatly for my personal tastes.<br/><br/>You still get such wonderful passages as this as they are preparing to drag an automobile through a muddy patch of road, which seems intentionally kept impassable just so the man with</span>
  <span id="freeText2173845798940883148" style="display:none">Not my favorite Faulkner, but certainly funnier and more light-hearted than most (a solid 3.5). It started off rather strong and comically with quite the memorable cast of characters, but the pace seemed kind of uneven about halfway through and the ending wrapped things up a little too neatly for my personal tastes.<br /><br />You still get such wonderful passages as this as they are preparing to drag an automobile through a muddy patch of road, which seems intentionally kept impassable just so the man with the mules can charge to help the few motorists that existed at that time:<blockquote>There was something dreamlike about it. Not nightmarish: just dreamlike---the peaceful, quiet, remote, sylvan, almost primeval setting of ooze and slime and jungle-growth and heat in which the very mules themselves, peacefully swishing and stamping at the teeming infinitesimal invisible myriad life which was the actual air we moved and breathed in, were not only unalienable but in fact curiously appropriate, being themselves biological dead-ends and hence already obsolete before they were born; the automobile: the expensive useless mechanical toy rated in power and strength by the dozens of horses, yet held helpless and impotent in the almost infantile clutch of a few inches of the temporary confederation of two mild and pacific elements---earth and water---which the frailest integers and units of motion as produced by the ancient unmechanical methods, had coped with for billions of generations without really having noticed it; the three of us, three forked identical and now unrecognizable mud-colored creatures engaged in a life-and-death struggle with it. the progress---if any---of which had to be computed in dreadful and glacier-like inches.</blockquote><br /><br />Keep in mind, our dear narrator, is an 11 yr. old, who helps "borrow" his grandfather's car (which is more novelty than a necessity at this point in history) and flees across state for a few days learning more than he ever cared to. He is wise beyond his years to begin with, but still has the normal concerns of a boy that age: "I never knew any ladies anywhere that wasn't trying to make somebody take a bath."<br /><br />Maybe I just couldn't handle Faulkner being this damn straightforward about a tale, but more realistically, I think, I was preferring he'd maintain the humor that delighted me through about half the book. As reviews like <a href="https://themillions.com/2015/03/getting-meta-about-mules-faulkner-and-the-fine-art-of-slowing-down.html" rel="nofollow noopener">"Getting Meta About Mules"</a> attest, this is not just the surface, sentimental road trip it appears to be.<br />-----------------------------<br />WORDS I'M PRETENDING I'LL REMEMBER JUST BECAUSE I LOOKED THEM UP ONCE<br /><a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/forrader" rel="nofollow noopener">forrader</a> | <a href="https://en.oxforddictionaries.com/definition/adumbrate" rel="nofollow noopener">adumbration</a> | pugnuckling | <a href="https://www.dictionary.com/browse/en-deshabille" rel="nofollow noopener">en deshabille</a> | <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/uxorious" rel="nofollow noopener">uxoriousness</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="2173845798940883148" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2520586303" />
    <a title="Tim" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/75270105-tim"><img alt="Tim" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1552249586p2/75270105.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/75270105-tim?shelf=pulitzer-prize-winners">pulitzer-prize-winners</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2520586303" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9762892721267667694">This book by William Faulkner won the Pulitzer Prize in 1963. The story is similar to a Huck Finn adventure when automobiles were first manufactured. The writing was a bit slow in progressing the story and the adventure was a bit improbable. However, I really liked the ending. I give this book 4 stars.</span>

          </span>

    <a title="Illiterate" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/72945589-illiterate"><img alt="Illiterate" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1539215959p2/72945589.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2926207141?book_show_action=true">Aug 08, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

<div id="review_932986945" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/932986945" />
    <a title="Sylvester" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2289972-sylvester"><img alt="Sylvester" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1267144286p2/2289972.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/932986945?book_show_action=true">May 10, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sylvester" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sylvester" href="/user/show/2289972-sylvester">Sylvester</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2289972-sylvester?shelf=animals">animals</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2289972-sylvester?shelf=picaresque">picaresque</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2289972-sylvester?shelf=adventure">adventure</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2289972-sylvester?shelf=classic">classic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2289972-sylvester?shelf=2014">2014</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2289972-sylvester?shelf=road-trip">road-trip</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer932986945" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9563560998885533002">3.5* (I may have liked this book more in the way of complete surprise - to find myself reading Faulkner without throwing the book down and stomping on it. If Joe Shmoe had written it, would I be less impressed?) Faulkner is a superb writer whose <i>way</i> of telling a story (backwards, forwards, fits and starts, parentheses within parentheses, switchbacks and a whole lotta beating around the bush) is bewildering and exasperating for the most part beyond bearing. When, however, he decides to tell a sto</span>
  <span id="freeText9563560998885533002" style="display:none">3.5* (I may have liked this book more in the way of complete surprise - to find myself reading Faulkner without throwing the book down and stomping on it. If Joe Shmoe had written it, would I be less impressed?) Faulkner is a superb writer whose <i>way</i> of telling a story (backwards, forwards, fits and starts, parentheses within parentheses, switchbacks and a whole lotta beating around the bush) is bewildering and exasperating for the most part beyond bearing. When, however, he decides to tell a story straight up, as in "Unvanquished", or this one here - he can be incredible.<br /><br />People keep calling this a "comedy", well, maybe in light of the usual Faulkner fare, but there is plenty of disturbing stuff going on - I almost wonder if F. isn't laying a trap for us. Who is going to laugh and then realize just what they were laughing at? <br /><br />There was a lot going on in this story, but for me, it was the question of innocence that kept screaming in my face - here is an 11 year old boy seeing, hearing, taking part in a lot of things illegal, adult and/or as he calls it "un-virtuous". Even before anything really happens, he says, <i>"When grown up people speak of the innocence of children, they don't really know what they mean. Pressed, they will go a step further and say, Well ignorance then. The child is neither. There is no crime which a boy of eleven had not envisaged long ago. His only innocence is, he may not yet be old enough to desire the fruits of it, which is not innocence but appetite; his ignorance is, he does not yet know how to commit it, which is not ignorance but size."</i> This theme crops up all over the place. How once he chose the path of "un-virtue", fellow conspirators seemed to line up to aid him. Faulkner continually underlines the fact that Lucius knows he's in over his head, but has simply gone too far to not see it through. However, Lucius has family - a grandfather especially, who has been down a few roads himself, and sees what is needed. (I think I can add this without spoiling the story.) <br /><br /><i>"There aint anything to do? Not anything?"<br />"I didn't say that," Grandfather said, "I said I couldn't. You can."<br />"What?" I said. "How can I forget it? Tell me how to."<br />"You can't," he said. "Nothing is ever forgotten. Nothing is ever lost. It's too valuable."<br />"Then what can I do?"<br />"Live with it," Grandfather said.<br />"Live with it? You mean forever? For the rest of my life? Not ever to get rid of it? Never? I can't. Don't you see I can't?"<br />"Yes you can," he said. "You will. A gentleman always does. A gentleman can live through anything. He faces anything. A gentleman accepts the responsibility of his actions and bears the burden of their consequences, even when he himself did not instigate them but only acquiesced to them, didn't say No though he knew he should. Come here." Then I was crying hard..."There," he said at last. "That should have emptied the cistern. Now go wash your face. A gentleman cries too, but he always washes his face."</i><br /><br />Yeah. That's right. Innocence to experience to innocence+experience, as my Prof used to say. There was a lot more in this book - a lot about race, for instance. (It might deserve another star, I'm thinking - but my rating system is so fatally flawed I don't know where to begin reform.) I enjoyed this book. I'm going to quit while I'm ahead, though. No more Faulkner for me.<br /><br />*new words<br />cumshaw<br />lagniappe<br />callipygian</span>
  <a data-text-id="9563560998885533002" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('NkWyKnSsN5rl0g5i4UACuHQBZKw700jQN5oMc2Y0yyKjep6FhWHqmC/OcjWvr0KAci6Gv6AbhI8SeYg/JpWgdA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('2bN57QZHeBU1PxvIRR3ZvQe/PQHIc1LH4C2zAwIaLSNMjFVC94qlF/8jZ58L8pmFAZDfElO7npjFzjdPQrtGdQ==')})
  }

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/3535.William_Faulkner">About William Faulkner</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/3535.William_Faulkner"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1437805575p3/3535.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>

<a href="/author/show/3535.William_Faulkner">William Faulkner
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>

followers

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer14496381759795665183">William Cuthbert Faulkner was a Nobel Prize-winning American novelist and short story writer. One of the most influential writers of the twentieth century, his reputation is based mostly on his novels, novellas, and short stories. He was also a published poet and an occasional screenwriter.<br/><br/>The majority of his works are set in his native state of Mississippi. Though his work was published as early </span>
  <span id="freeText14496381759795665183" style="display:none">William Cuthbert Faulkner was a Nobel Prize-winning American novelist and short story writer. One of the most influential writers of the twentieth century, his reputation is based mostly on his novels, novellas, and short stories. He was also a published poet and an occasional screenwriter.<br /><br />The majority of his works are set in his native state of Mississippi. Though his work was published as early as 1919, and largely during the 1920s and 1930s, Faulkner was relatively unknown until receiving the 1949 Nobel Prize in Literature, "for his powerful and artistically unique contribution to the modern American novel." Faulkner has often been cited as one of the most important writers in the history of American literature. Faulkner was influenced by European modernism, and employed stream of consciousness in several of his novels.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14496381759795665183" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/3127511-the-reivers">Quotes from The Reivers</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;It was too late. Maybe yesterday, while I was still a child, but not now. I knew too much, had seen too much, I was a child no longer now; innocence and childhood were forever lost, forever gone from me.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/711272-it-was-too-late-maybe-yesterday-while-i-was-still">13 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;I lied," I said. ...<br />"I know it," he said.<br />"Then do something about it. Do anything, just so it's something."<br />"I cant," he said.<br />"There aint anything to do? Not anything?"<br />"I didn't say that," Grandfather said. "I said I couldn't. You can."<br />"What?" I said. "How can I forget it? Tell me how to."<br />"You cant," he said. "Nothing is ever forgotten. Nothing is ever lost. It's too valuable."<br />"Then what can I do?"<br />"Live with it," Grandfather said.<br />"Live with it? You mean, forever? For the rest of my life? Not ever to get rid of it? Never? I cant. Dont you see that I cant?"<br />"Yes you can," he said. "You will. A gentleman always does. A gentleman can live through anything. He faces anything. A gentleman accepts the responsibility of his actions and bears the burden of their consequences, even when he did not himself instigate them but only acquiesced to them, didn't say No though he knew he should.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/711283-i-lied-i-said-i-know-it-he-said">12 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>

a call to SignInPromptController#get to determine if the user should see the sign in interstial.

last seen the insterstitial.  If the controller responds indicating the popup should appear, the
